الشارع جیسے تیسویں رمضان کے صوم کا واجب اور حسین ہونا اور یکم شوال کے صوم کا قبیح ہونا معلوم بالعقل نہیں اس کا علم اور ادراک صرف شریعت سے ہوتا ہے۔ فالاول لا يكون من الفقہ پہلے قسم کے افعال جن کا حسن و قبح عقل سے معلوم ہوتا ہے یہ علم الفقہ میں درج نہیں ہیں بلکہ یہ علم الاخلاق کہلاتا ہے والثاني هو الفقہ یعنی دوسرے قسم کے افعال جن کا حسن قبح عقلی نہیں بلکہ شرعی ہے یہ ہے فقہ اصطلاحی تو اس مذہب کے لحاظ سے فقہ کی صد صحیح اور جامع مانع ہو جائے گی ۔ واما عند الاشعرى واتباعه كالشوافع نحن كل شئ او یعنی اشاعره کے نزدیک ہرشی کا حسن و قبح شرعی ہے یعنی موقوف علی الشرع ہے اور عقلی نہیں ہے تو یہ دونوں قسم کے افعال ان کے نزدیک فقہ میں درج ہوں گے۔ مع ان حسن التواضع یعنی تواضع وجود کا حسن اور ان کے اضداد کا قبیح یہ فقہ اصطلاحی میں کسی کے نزدیک درج نہیں ہیں لیکن اشاعرہ کے نزدیک چونکہ یہ احکام شرعیہ ہیں عقلیہ نہیں ہیں اس لئے ان کے مذہب پر فقہ اصطلاحی میں داخل ہو جائیں گے لہذا مذہب اشعریہ پر یہ تعریف فقہ صحیح نہیں ہوگی۔ ولا يزاد علیه ای على حد الفقه المصطلح - التي لا يعدو كونها من الدین ضرورة یعنی فقہ اصطلاحی کی تعریف مذکور پر التی لا یعلم الم کی قید کا اضافہ اس غرض کے ماتحت کہ علم بالصلوٰۃ والصوم فقہ اصطلاحی سے خارج ہو جائے کیونکہ ان کا من الدین ہونا بداہت معلوم ہے اس کی ضرورت نہیں ہے کیونکہ بعض احکام کے علم کو فقہ نہیں کہا جاتا۔ اعلم ان ھذا القیاد اس کے اضافہ نہ کرنے کی مزید تفصیل کہ رہا ہے اصل میں اس کا ذکر امام رازی نے اپنی کتاب المحصول میں کیا ہے تاکہ وہ مسائل جن کا دین سے ہونا بداھتہ ہر شخص کو معلوم ہے ان کا علم فقہ اصطلاحی سے خارج ہو جائے اگر یہ قید نہ کی جائے تو پھر لازم آتا ہے کہ وہ شخص جو ان کے وجوب کا عالم ہے وہ فقیہ ہو جائے حالانکہ وہ فقیہ نہیں ہے۔ فاقول هذا القيد ضائع یہ مصنف اس قید کے اضافہ پر ردکر رہا ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ یہ قید بالکل صنائع ہے اور اس کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ لانا لا نسلو یعنی ہم تسلیم ہی نہیں کرتے کہ ان لو لم يخرج یعنی یہ قید ذکر کر کے اگر اسے خارج نہ کیا جائے تو ان کے وجوب کا عالم فقیہ ہو جائے گا ہم اس کو تسلیم نہیں کرتے لان المواد اس لئے کہ مراد بالاحکام بعض تو نہیں ہیں وان قل اگر چہ قلیل ہوں کیونکہ ایک شخص سو مسائل کو ادلہ سے جانتا ہے خواہ ان کا دین سے ہونا بدا حقہ معلوم ہو یا نہ ہو جیسے کتاب الرمین کے غریبہ مسائل کو جانتا ہو یہ بھی فقیہ نہیں ہو سکتا تو صوم وصلوٰۃ کے عالم کو فقیہ کیسے کہا جاسکتا ہے لہذا اس عذر فاسد کی بنیاد پر اس قید کا اضافہ کر کے علم بوجوب الصوم والصلوۃ کو خارج کرنا صیح نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ ضائع اور بلا ضرورت ہے ۔ مصنف کے قول وان قل پر یہ وہم کیا گیا ہے کہ اس کی بجائے وان گشتر ہوتا یعنی اگر چہ کثیر مسائل کا اسے علم ہو تو بھی فقیہ نہیں ہوگا تو اس وہم کا دفع یوں کیا جاتا ہے کہ اس کو ظاہر پر محمول کرنے سے وہم پیدا ہوتا ہے حالانکہ یہ اپنے مابعد کے ساتھ مل کر دلیل بنتا ہے مقصد یہ ہے کہ عالم بمانة مسألة من ادلتہا بھی فقیہ نہیں ہو سکتا فكيف يكون العالم بمسائل قليلة فقيها گویا کہ وان کو بمعنی کیف کر دیا گیا معنی ہو کیف يكون العالم بالقليل فقيها - ثواعلم انه لا يراد فقه کی تعریف مذکور پہ اعتراض ہے کہ احکام سے کتنا مقدار مراد ہوگی حاصل یہ ہے کہ احکام سے کل من حیث الکل یعنی مجموع احکام جو الی یوم القیامہ ہونے والے ہیں یہ تو مراد نہیں ہو سکتے۔ لان الحوادث لا تكاد تتناهی یعنی یہ ہونے والے حوادثات تو کہیں رکتے ہی نہیں روزمرہ نئے نئے مسائل پیدا ہوتے رہیں گے تو ان تمام کو علم انسان محیط نہیں ہو سکتا۔ ولا ضابط یعنی کوئی ضابطہ کلیہ بھی نہیں ہے جو ان تمام کو جمع کرے تاکہ اس ضابطہ کے ساتھ ان کا علم حاصل ہو سکے لہذا یہ شقی تو صحیح نہیں ہے ولا يواد كل واحد یعنی ہر ایک ایک مسئلہ جو مجتہد کے زندگی میں اس کے سامنے آئے اس کا علم مراد ہو تو یہ بھی نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ مجتہدین کو اپنی زندگی میں ایسے مسائل پیش آتے ہیں جن کے جواب میں انہوں
Which cannot be perceived and known without the address of the Lawgiver. The conclusion is that by 'shar'i' is meant what depends on the initiation [of the Lawgiver], otherwise ruling and 'shar'i' would have one meaning and the word 'shar'i' would be redundant. It is equal whether the address occurs exactly in that ruling or occurs in a situation to which that ruling is in need, like analogical issues because in analogical issues the address of the Lawgiver does not occur exactly in that analogical ruling, but that address occurs in the analogized base to which the analogically derived ruling is in need. If the address of the Lawgiver does not occur in the analogized base, then knowledge and perception of the analogical ruling would not be possible. Hence, analogical rulings are also shar'i rulings. So this enters the definition of fiqh. This is an objection to the mentioned definition of fiqh: that for those who deny and reject rational good and evil, every good and evil would enter into the definition of fiqh. Know that 'indana' (in our view) is the detail of this mentioned objection, the gist of which is: In our view, i.e., the Hanafis who are called Maturidis, and in the view of the majority of the Mu'tazila, some actions have rational good and evil, meaning that the knowledge and perception of their good and evil does not depend on the Shari'ah, rather their knowledge and perception can be with reason, like the goodness of humility etc., and some not, meaning some actions are such that their good and evil cannot be known by reason, but must depend on the address of the Lawgiver, like the obligation and goodness of fasting on the 30th of Ramadan and the ugliness of fasting on the 1st of Shawwal. This is not known by reason; its knowledge and perception only come from the Shari'ah. So the first [type] is not from fiqh: the actions of the first kind whose good and evil are known by reason are not included in the knowledge of fiqh, rather this is called ethics. The second is fiqh: meaning the second kind of actions whose good and evil are not rational but shar'i, this is terminological fiqh. Thus according to this school, the definition of fiqh becomes correct and comprehensive. As for according to al-Ash'ari and his followers like the Shafi'is, every thing's good and evil is shar'i, meaning dependent on the Shari'ah and not rational, so both kinds of actions will be included in fiqh in their view. Although the goodness of humility and the ugliness of its opposites are not included in terminological fiqh in anyone's view, but according to the Ash'aris, since these rulings are shar'i not rational, therefore on their school they would enter into terminological fiqh. Hence, on the Ash'ari school this definition of fiqh is not correct. And let not be added to it, i.e., to the definition of terminological fiqh, the condition 'which do not exceed being from religion by necessity', meaning on the mentioned definition of terminological fiqh, adding the condition 'which are not known from religion necessarily' for the purpose that knowledge of prayer and fasting would be excluded from terminological fiqh because their being from religion is known inherently; there is no need for it. Because the knowledge of some rulings is not called fiqh. Know that this addition not to be made is a further detail of it. Originally its mention is by Imam al-Razi in his book al-Mahsul, so that issues whose being from religion is inherently known to every person, their knowledge would be excluded from terminological fiqh. If this condition is not made, then it entails that the person who knows their obligation would become a faqih, whereas he is not a faqih. So I say: this condition is useless, the author responds to the addition of this condition. The summary is that this condition is completely spurious and there is no need for it. Because we do not concede that if they do not exclude [by this condition], then the one who knows their obligation becomes a faqih. We do not concede that. Because the intent by 'rulings' is not some, even if few, because a person who knows a hundred issues from their proofs, whether their being from religion is known inherently or not, like he knows the obscure issues of the Book of Rentals, he still cannot be a faqih. So how can one who knows prayer and fasting be called a faqih? Hence, on the basis of this invalid excuse, adding this condition to exclude knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting is not correct, rather it is useless and unnecessary. Regarding the author's statement 'wa in qalla' there is a misunderstanding that its alternative is 'wa in kathura', i.e., even if he has knowledge of many issues, he still would not be a faqih. So the refutation of this misunderstanding is done by that it causes misunderstanding when applied to the apparent, whereas it forms a proof by connecting with what follows it. The purpose is that one who knows even one issue from its proofs cannot be a faqih, so how can one who knows few issues be a faqih? Thus 'wa in' is taken in the meaning of 'kayfa', meaning: how can one who knows few issues be a faqih? Then know that it is not intended, to the mentioned definition of fiqh, there is an objection: what quantity is meant by 'rulings'? The gist is that 'rulings' cannot mean the whole of all rulings that will occur until the Day of Judgment, because events are almost endless; daily new issues arise, so human knowledge cannot encompass all of them. Nor is there a universal rule that collects them all so that knowledge of them could be obtained through that rule. Hence this aspect is not correct. Nor is each single issue that comes before a mujtahid in his lifetime meant to be known, because that also cannot be, as mujtahids face issues in their lives in response to which they...
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the definition of fiqh. It begins by establishing that shar'i rulings include those derived through analogy (qiyas), even though the textual address is not directly on the analogized ruling but on the original case. This is because analogical reasoning depends on the Lawgiver's address. The commentator then addresses an objection: if one holds the Ash'ari view that all good and evil are determined only by revelation (i.e., no rational good and evil), then every action's moral value would be a shar'i ruling and thus would enter the definition of fiqh. This would make the definition of fiqh too broad, including even ethical virtues like humility, which are not typically considered fiqh. The passage explains the Hanafi (Maturidi) position: some actions have rational good and evil (e.g., humility is known to be good by reason), while others depend solely on revelation (e.g., fasting on specific days). Only the latter constitute fiqh. Thus the definition of fiqh is sound for Hanafis but problematic for Ash'aris. The text then discusses a proposal to add a condition to the definition of fiqh: 'which are not known from the religion by necessity' to exclude obvious matters like the obligation of prayer and fasting. The commentator refutes this, arguing that knowledge of even many detailed issues does not make one a faqih; rather, fiqh is the knowledge of rulings through evidence, not mere knowledge of obligations. The addition is deemed unnecessary and deceptive. Finally, the passage raises an objection about the scope of 'rulings' in the definition: it cannot mean all possible rulings (since they are infinite) nor each individual case that arises, as this is impractical. The discussion continues into the next page.

Key Terms
فقه (fiqh): Knowledge of shar'i rulings derived from their specific evidences, excluding matters known by reason or by necessity from religion
حسن و قبح (husn wa qubh): Good and evil of actions; for Hanafis (Maturidis) some are rational (known by reason) and some are shar'i (known by revelation)
شرعي (shar'i): That which depends on the address of the Lawgiver (Shari'), whether directly or through qiyas

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi (Maturidi) position, which of the following is an example of an action whose good/evil is known only through revelation (shar'i)?
A) Humility towards others
B) Fasting on the 30th of Ramadan
C) Truthfulness in speech
D) Justice in dealings

Key Takeaway
Fiqh in the Hanafi school is knowledge of rulings that depend on revelation, excluding those known by reason or by religious necessity.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
12 min
Refuting a Restriction in the Definition of Fiqh
Refuting a proposed restriction in the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the addition of a restriction excluding obviously known rulings from the definition of fiqh, and identify the correct scope of 'ahkam' in the definition.

Core Idea
A restriction to exclude obvious rulings is redundant; fiqh is knowledge derived from detailed evidence, not mere awareness.

The definition of fiqh has been a subject of scholarly debate. One proposal by Imam al-Razi adds a restriction to exclude rulings known with necessity, like the obligation of prayer. However, Hanafi scholars argue that this addition is unnecessary. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi refutation and analyze what quantity of rulings 'ahkam' actually refers to in the definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute
Identify the restriction proposed by Imam al-Razi and its purpose: to exclude rulings known by necessity (like prayer's obligation) to avoid calling a common believer a faqih.
This restriction appears in his work Al-Mahsul.
2
Grasp the Hanafi objection
The definition already requires knowledge from detailed evidence, so a person who knows a ruling without evidence is not a faqih; the restriction is redundant and wasteful.
Hanafi counterexample: one who knows 100 obscure issues from evidence is not automatically a faqih.
3
Analyze the scope of 'ahkam'
The passage rejects several interpretations of what 'ahkam' means in the definition: (a) all rulings until Judgment Day (too vast), (b) every single issue encountered (contradicted by 'I don't know' responses), (c) a fraction or majority (unknown total), (d) potential to know (remote or undefined), (e) knowing every ruling through ijtihad (impossible due to error and issues without ijtihad scope).
The example of Imam Abu Hanifa's tawaqquf on 'al-dahr' is given.
4
Conclude the Hanafi position
Fiqh is knowledge of a finite set of rulings derived from detailed evidence, and the restriction is unnecessary. The definition stands without the proposed addition.
The commentator calls the restriction 'dai'i' (wasteful).

علم حاصل ہو سکے لہذا یہ شق تو صحیح نہیں ہے ولا يواد كل واحد یعنی ہر ایک ایک مسئلہ جو مجتہد کے زندگی میں اس کے سامنے آئے اس کا علم مراد ہو تو یہ بھی نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ مجتہدین کو اپنی زندگی میں ایسے مسائل پیش آتے ہیں جن کے جواب میں انہوں نے لا ادری کہا ہے حالانکہ وہ بالاتفاق فقیہ ہیں تو معلوم ہوا کہ یہ شق بھی صحیح نہیں ہے ولا بعض له نسبة اليمنى بعض جس کو نسبة معينہ الی الکل ہو مثلا کل مسائل کی نصف وثلث وغیرہ یہ بھی مراد نہیں ہو سکتی اور اسی طرح اکثر مسائل مراد لینا بھی صحیح نہیں کیونکہ جب مقدار کل مجہول ہے تو اس کا بعض معین یا اکثر مراد لینا صحیح نہیں ہو سکتا ۔ ولا التھیو للکل یعنی کل مسائل کے جاننے کا تھیو یعنی صلاحیت و استعداد بھی مراد نہیں ہو سکتی کیونکہ تھیو بعید تو غیر فقیہ کو بھی حاصل ہے اور تھیو قریب مجہول ہے اس کا کوئی ضابطہ نہیں ہے لہذا یہ شق بھی صحیح نہیں ہے ولا يرا دانه يكون بحيث الا اور یہ بھی مراد نہیں ہو سکتی کہ اجتہاد کے ساتھ ہر ایک مسئلہ کا حکم جان لیوے کیونکہ علماء مجتہدین ایسے بھی ہیں کہ نہ ندگی بھر بعض احکام معلوم نہیں کر سکے۔ کالی حنيفة لم يدر الدهر یعنی جب کوئی شخص کہتا ہے کہ واللہ لا کلمہ الدھر تو ادھر سے کتنا مدت مراد ہے تو امام ابوحنیفہ تمام زندگی اس کا حکم معلوم نہ کر سکے اس لئے اس مسئلہ میں توقف فرمایا وللخطاء في الاجتھاد اور اجتہاد میں غلطی اور خطا کا امکان ہے ولان حکم اور بعض حوادث کے احکام ایسے ہوتے ہیں کہ ان کے اندر اجتہاد کی مساغ و گنجائش نہیں ہوتی تو پھر اجتہاد کے ساتھ کیسے معلوم کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ وايضا لا يليق في الحد اور صدر تعریف میں آمنا سب اور لائق نہیں ہے کہ لفظ علم ذکر کر کے اس سے تھیو مخصوص لیا جائے کیونکہ لفظ علم تھیو مخصوص پر دلالت نہیں کرتا جب یہ تمام شقوق باطل ہوئے تو ضروری ہے کہ فقہ ایک جملہ احکام متناہیہ اور مضبوط کا علم ہو ۔

قال الشارح في التلويح قوله والشرعية ما لا يدرك لولا خطاب الشارع بنفس الحكم لعني خطاب شارخ خواه نفس حکم میں وارد ہو یا اصل حکم میں ایسا اصل جو حکم کے لئے مقیس علیہ کا درجہ رکھتا ہے تو جو خطاب مقیس علیہ میں وارد ہے اسی کا ورود حکم مقیس میں معتبر قرار دیا جاتا ہے تو جو احکام اس قسم کے خطاب پر موقوف ہیں اور ان کے بغیر ان احکام کا ادراک نہ ہو سکے تو اسے شرعیہ کہا جاتا ہے اور قید شرعیہ کے ساتھ فقہ کی تعریف سے وہ احکام نکل جائیں گے جو موقوف على الشرع نہیں ہیں مثل وجوب الایمان - ويدخل مثل كون الاجماع یعنی اجماع اور قیاس کا حجت ہونا بشر طیکہ یہ احکام ہوں یہ شرعیہ میں داخل رہیں گے وانما لوفيس الشرعية یعنی شد ما ورد به خطاب الشرع کے ساتھ نہیں کی وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ جب حکم کا معنی خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ مراد ہے تو یہ ہم معنیٰ ماورد بہ خطاب الشرع کے ہے اگر شرعیہ کا مفہوم بھی یہی ہو تو پھر شرعیہ کی قید کرد ہوگی اور اس کا کوئی فائدہ معتد بہا نہیں ہوگا۔ وعند الاشاعرة یعنی اشاعرہ کے نزدیک چونکہ حسن و قبح عقلی ہے اس لئے ان کے نزدیک فقہ کی تعریف میں شرعیہ کی قید ضروری ہے۔
For the purpose that knowledge of prayer and fasting should be excluded from terminological fiqh, because their being from the religion is known with necessity, this is not needed, because knowledge of some rulings is not called fiqh. Know that this restriction is giving further detail of not adding it; originally Imam Razi mentioned it in his book Al-Mahsul, so that those issues whose being from the religion is known to everyone with necessity, their knowledge should be excluded from terminological fiqh. If this restriction is not made, then it would necessitate that a person who knows their obligation becomes a faqih, whereas he is not a faqih.

So I say: This restriction is wasteful. The author is rejecting the addition of this restriction; the gist is that this restriction is completely wasteful and there is no need for it. For we do not accept that if it is not excluded, then the one who knows their obligation would become a faqih; we do not accept that, because the rulings intended are not some (particular ones), even if few. Because a person who knows a hundred issues from evidence, whether their being from the religion is known with necessity or not, like he knows the obscure issues of the book of oaths, he too cannot be a faqih, so how can the one who knows prayer and fasting be called a faqih? Therefore, on the basis of this invalid excuse, adding this restriction to exclude knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting is not correct; rather it is wasteful and unnecessary. Regarding the author's saying 'wa in qalla', it was mistakenly thought that it should be 'wa in kathura', meaning even if he has knowledge of many issues he would not be a faqih. The refutation of this misconception is that interpreting it literally creates a misunderstanding, whereas it forms a proof together with what follows; the intent is that the one who knows even one issue from its evidence cannot be a faqih, so how can the one who knows few issues be a faqih? Thus 'wa in' is taken in the meaning of 'kayfa', so the meaning is: how can the one who knows few be a faqih?

Then know that it is not intended... There is an objection to the mentioned definition of fiqh: what quantity of rulings is meant? The conclusion is that the totality of rulings as a whole, i.e., all rulings that will occur until the Day of Judgment, cannot be intended, because events are nearly endless; daily new issues arise, so human knowledge cannot encompass them all. Nor is there a universal principle that collects them all so that knowledge of them can be obtained through that principle, so this possibility is invalid. Nor is it intended to mean every single issue that a mujtahid encounters in his lifetime, because mujtahids in their lifetime encounter issues to which they replied 'I do not know,' yet they are unanimously considered fuqaha'; so it is clear that this possibility is also invalid. Nor is it a part that has a specific ratio to the whole, e.g., half or one-third of all issues, nor can the majority be intended, because when the total is unknown, intending a specific part or the majority cannot be correct. Nor is the thuyu (potential/capacity) to know all issues intended, because remote potential is also possessed by a non-faqih, and near potential is unknown and has no principle; so this possibility is also invalid. Nor is it intended that he should be such that he knows the ruling of every issue through ijtihad, because there are mujtahid scholars who throughout their lives could not know some rulings, like Abu Hanifa who did not know 'al-dahr' (the time duration meant in an oath): when a person says 'By Allah, I will not speak to him al-dahr,' he could not know the ruling for his entire life, so he refrained (tawaqquf) on this issue. And there is the possibility of error in ijtihad, and some rulings of events have no room for ijtihad. Also, it is not suitable in the definition (that we should take from it the meaning of potential), and it is not appropriate that after mentioning the word 'ilm' (knowledge) in the beginning of the definition, it should be taken as meaning specific potential, because the word 'ilm' does not indicate specific potential. When all these possibilities are invalid, it is necessary that fiqh is knowledge of a finite and definite set of rulings.

The commentator said in Al-Talwih: His saying 'and the shari'iyya (legal rulings) are those that cannot be perceived without the address of the Lawgiver' means the address of the Lawgiver, whether it occurs in the ruling itself or in the original ruling (asl) that serves as the maqis alayh (basis for analogy). So the address that occurs in the maqis alayh is considered to be a valid occurrence in the maqis (derived ruling). Therefore, rulings that depend on this kind of address and cannot be perceived without them are called shari'iyya. With the restriction 'shari'iyya', those rulings that are not dependent on the Lawgiver, such as the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman), are excluded from the definition of fiqh. And included are rulings like that ijma' and qiyas are proofs (hujjiyya), provided that these are rulings; they will be included in the shari'iyya. The reason for not saying 'what the address of the Lawgiver has come with' is that when the meaning of 'hukm' (ruling) is taken as the address of Allah Most High, it is synonymous with 'what the address of the Lawgiver has come with'. If the meaning of shari'iyya is also the same, then the restriction 'shari'iyya' would be redundant and have no significant benefit. According to the Ash'aris, since good and evil (husn and qubh) are rational, they consider the restriction 'shari'iyya' in the definition of fiqh necessary.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the definition of fiqh from a Hanafi commentary on Al-Tawdih wa Al-Talwih. The author begins by addressing an objection raised by Imam al-Razi in his Al-Mahsul, who added a restriction to the definition to exclude rulings that are obviously known from the religion, such as the obligation of prayer and fasting. The reasoning was that without this restriction, anyone who knows these obvious obligations would be considered a faqih, which is not the case. The commentator refutes this by arguing that the definition already requires knowledge from detailed evidence; a person who knows a hundred issues from evidence, even obscure ones, is still not a faqih if he does not have the necessary qualifications. Thus, the mere knowledge of a few obvious rulings does not make one a faqih, so the additional restriction is unnecessary and wasteful.

Then the passage analyzes what quantity of rulings is intended by 'ahkam' in the definition. It systematically rejects several possibilities: (1) all rulings until the Day of Judgment (impossible because they are infinite and no universal principle encompasses them), (2) every single issue encountered by a mujtahid (because mujtahids sometimes say 'I do not know' and are still fuqaha), (3) a specific fraction of all rulings (unknown total makes this arbitrary), (4) the majority of rulings (similarly unknown), (5) the potential (thuyu) to know all rulings (because potential is either remote—present even in non-fuqaha—or near—which is undefined), and (6) the ability to know every ruling through ijtihad (because mujtahids can err and some issues have no room for ijtihad, as exemplified by Imam Abu Hanifa's tawaqquf on the ruling of 'al-dahr' in an oath). The conclusion is that fiqh must be knowledge of a finite, definite set of rulings that a mujtahid actually acquires through detailed evidence.

Finally, the passage discusses the term 'shari'iyya' in the definition, explaining that it refers to rulings that cannot be known without the address of the Lawgiver, either directly or through analogy. This excludes rationally known obligations like the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman). The commentator also notes that the Ash'ari school requires this restriction because they hold that good and evil are rational, so without 'shari'iyya', fiqh would include rational judgments. The Hanafi position, however, does not require an explicit restriction because the definition already implies derivation from divine address.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge of legal rulings (ahkam shar'iyya 'amaliyya) acquired from their detailed evidence.
الأحكام (al-ahkam): The legal rulings (e.g., obligation, prohibition) that are the subject matter of fiqh. The passage analyzes what quantity is meant: it must be a finite set that a mujtahid can actually know.
التھیو (al-thuyu (or al-qabiliyya)): Potential or capacity to know rulings; the passage distinguishes between remote potential (held even by non-fuqaha) and near potential (which is undefined and thus not suitable for the definition).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the restriction to exclude obviously known rulings (like the obligation of prayer) from the definition of fiqh considered unnecessary?
A) Because such rulings are not part of fiqh at all.
B) Because the definition already requires knowledge from detailed evidence, which such a person lacks.
C) Because Imam Abu Hanifa never accepted such restrictions.
D) Because the restriction makes the definition too broad.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fiqh does not require a separate restriction for obvious rulings; it properly identifies a faqih as one who derives rulings from detailed evidence.

Fiqh Excludes Internal States: A Hanafi Defense
Defending the author's view that fiqh deals with external actions, not internal character traits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the Hanafi school distinguishes fiqh from akhlaq (internal moral qualities) and apply that distinction to evaluate whether an action falls within the scope of fiqh.

Core Idea
Fiqh governs external actions (af‘āl jawāriḥ); internal character traits are matters of the heart, not of legal science.

This passage clarifies a crucial boundary in the Hanafi definition of fiqh. The commentator mistakenly thought that acts like comforting an orphan would be included, thus making the definition not preventive. The author defends the definition by showing that such acts, though commanded, are not discussed in fiqh books and therefore fall outside the science. Understanding this distinction is essential for knowing exactly what fiqh as a discipline studies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commentator's objection
Recognize that the commentator claimed the author's definition of fiqh was not preventive because acts of generosity (like comforting an orphan) should be included but are absent from fiqh books.
The objection hinges on the inclusion of external acts that are effects of internal traits.
2
Distinguish internal traits from external effects
The author clarifies that 'generosity' and 'humility' in the definition refer to outward effects (e.g., showing kindness), not the internal moral qualities themselves. These effects are physical actions of the limbs (af‘āl jawāriḥ).
Internal traits (akhlāq bāṭinah) are not the subject of fiqh.
3
Recognize the Hanafi exclusion
Understand that fiqh, as a science, only discusses certain external actions that have specific legal rulings and are systematically covered in fiqh works. Actions like verbal comfort or patting an orphan's head, though commendable, are not part of that coverage.
Financial charity is an exception because it is discussed in fiqh under zakāh.
4
Compare with the Ash'ari position
Note that the Ash'aris (Shāfi'īs) consider all practical legal rulings, including compassionate acts, as part of fiqh. This leads to a broader definition that includes such actions.
This ikhtilaf is a legitimate difference in defining the subject matter of fiqh.
5
Conclude why the Hanafi definition remains valid
The author shows that the commentator's objection fails because the definition is indeed preventive: it excludes actions not covered in fiqh works. The Ash'ari view does not invalidate the Hanafi definition; it simply reflects a different scope.
The tarjih (preferred view) in this text is the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موجود ہے اس سے مصنف کو ذھول ہو گیا ہے۔ قیل لم نیس ولم يذهل المصنف بل الشارح لم يتدبر في كلام المصنف کیونکہ مصنف کی مراد جود اور تواضع سے اخلاق باطنہ اور ملکات نفسانیہ نہیں ہیں بلکہ ان کے آثار صادرہ مراد ہیں جو از قبیلہ افعال جوارح ہیں مثلاً عملی طور پر اظہار تواضع اور کیسی غریب و یتیم کے ساتھ عملی اظہار ہمدردی اور اظہار شفقت اور لسانی حیثیت سے ان کو تسکین و تسلی دینا یہ سب عملیات ہیں لیکن یہ فقہ میں درج نہیں ہیں لیکن اشاعرہ کے نزدیک یہ سب احکام شرعیہ عملیہ ہو کہ فقہ میں داخل ہو رہے لہذا تعریف مانع نہ رہی اگر چہ جود کے آثار میں سے صدقات مالیہ کا ادا کرنا تو فقہ میں درج ہے لیکن جسمی اعمال ہمدردانہ یہ بھی تو ان ملکات کے آثار ہیں جن سے فقہ بحث نہیں کرتی جیسے حدیث شریف میں یتیم کے سرپر دست شفقت رکھنے کی فضیلت مذکور ہے یہ بھی تو از قسم عمل ہے لیکن فقہ کے مباحث میں درج نہیں ہے اس قسم کے اعمال تعریف فقہ میں داخل ہو جائیں گے لیکن فقہ اصطلاحی نہیں ہیں تو منعیت تعریف منتقض ہو جائے گی واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
It exists; the author became heedless of this. It was said: no, the author did not become heedless; rather the commentator did not reflect on the author's words. Because the author's intention by generosity and humility is not the internal moral qualities and psychic traits, but rather their outward effects are intended, which are of the type of actions of the limbs. For example, practically showing humility, and practically showing compassion and kindness to the poor and orphan, and comforting them verbally. All these are actions, but they are not included in fiqh. However, according to the Ash'aris, all these are practical legal rulings, so they enter fiqh, so the definition is not preventive. Even though paying financial charity among the effects of generosity is included in fiqh, but such compassionate bodily actions are also effects of those traits which fiqh does not discuss. Like in the noble hadith, the virtue of placing a hand of kindness on the head of an orphan is mentioned; this too is an act, but it is not included in the discussions of fiqh. Actions of this type would enter the definition of fiqh, but they are not terminological fiqh, so the prevention of the definition would be invalidated. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement where the commentator (sharh) mistakenly thought the author (musannif) became heedless about including acts of generosity in fiqh. The author clarifies that fiqh, in its terminological sense as a science, deals with external physical actions (af'āl jawāriḥ), not internal character traits (akhlāq bāṭinah) or their outward effects like comforting an orphan. The Ash'aris include such actions as practical rulings, but the Hanafi definition limits fiqh to actions like paying charity. The hadith about patting an orphan's head is an example of a virtuous act not discussed in fiqh, showing that such general acts do not fall under its scope.

Key Terms
اخلاق باطنہ (akhlāq bāṭinah): Internal moral qualities or character traits, e.g., generosity, humility.
ملکات نفسانیہ (malakāt nafsāniyyah): Psychic traits or ingrained habits of the soul.
افعال جوارح (af‘āl jawāriḥ): Actions performed by the limbs, i.e., physical external actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author argue that the commentator's objection about including generosity in fiqh is invalid?
A) Because generosity is only about financial charity, which is already in fiqh.
B) Because the author meant external effects like showing humility, not internal traits, and such effects are not part of fiqh in the Hanafi view.
C) Because fiqh includes all actions, both internal and external, so the objection is unnecessary.
D) Because the Ash'aris' view is incorrect, and fiqh cannot include any action of the heart.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh as a discipline is defined by its subject matter (external actions) and its established content; not every commanded action automatically falls within its scope.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Why 'Ahkam' Cannot Mean All Rulings: A Definitional Critique
Critique of the Ash'ari definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five possible interpretations of 'ahkam' in the definition of fiqh and explain why each is invalid, thereby recognizing that fiqh must be defined as knowledge of the method (usul).

Core Idea
Fiqh cannot be knowledge of all rulings; it is knowledge of the method to derive rulings.

The Ash'ari school defined fiqh as 'knowledge of rulings' (al-ʿilm bi-l-aḥkām). But what does 'aḥkām' refer to? If it means all rulings, then no one can be a faqih. If it means some rulings, which ones? This passage systematically eliminates five possible meanings, clearing the ground for the correct definition: fiqh is knowledge of the sources and methods that yield rulings.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Ash'ari Definition
The Ash'aris define fiqh as 'knowledge of the shar'i rulings' (al-ʿilm bi-l-aḥkām al-sharʿiyya). Your first step is to note that the term 'aḥkām' is ambiguous and must be analyzed.
This definition is the target of the critique.
2
List the Five Possible Meanings of 'Ahkam'
The passage enumerates four/five possibilities: (1) the totality (al-kull), (2) each individual ruling (kull wāḥid), (3) some with a specific proportion (baʿḍ lahu nisba muʿayyana), (4) some absolutely (al-baʿḍ muṭlaqan). A fifth possibility (the '' method) is implicit. List them clearly.
The text says 'all these possibilities are invalid'.
3
Evaluate the First Possibility (Totality)
The passage argues that knowing all rulings is impossible because events (ḥawādith) are extremely numerous and never cease as long as the world exists. They cannot be fully encompassed by any mujtahid. Therefore, the totality cannot be intended.
The author clarifies that 'almost endless' refers to practical impossibility, not metaphysical infinitude.
4
Analyze the Remaining Possibilities
The second possibility (each ruling) would require a faqih to know every single ruling—also impossible. The third (a specific proportion) has no fixed ratio in fiqh. The fourth (some absolutely, even few) would allow anyone with minimal knowledge to be called a faqih, which is unacceptable. All are rejected.
The fifth possibility (the method) is the correct one, though not stated here explicitly.
5
Derive the Correct Definition
Since 'aḥkām' cannot be interpreted as the rulings themselves, the definition must be understood differently. Fiqh is knowledge of the evidence and methods (uṣūl) by which rulings are derived. This is the Hanafi position.
This conclusion underpins the entire science of uṣūl al-fiqh.

ثم اعتراضه لا يراد بالاحکام اعتراض یعنی اشاعرہ جو تعریف فقہ کی تھی کہ علم بالا حکام یہ اسی پر اعتراض ہے بان المواد یعنی احکام سے مراد اما الکل ای المجموع یعنی مجموع من حيث المجموع اما كل واحد یعنی فقیہ کے لئے لازم ہے کہ ہر ایک ایک حکم معلوم کرے۔ اما بعض له نسبة معينة يعنى بعض احكام مراد ہوں جن کو کل احکام کے ساتھ نسبت معینہ مخصوصہ حاصل ہو مثلاً نصف یا اکثر جیسے دو تہائی اما البعض مطلقا یا بعض مطلق مراد ہو خواہ قلیل ہی کیوں نہ ہوں والاقسام باسرها باطلة اور یہ تمام اقسام محتملہ مذکورہ باطل ہیں اما الاول فلان الحوادث یعنی کل مجموعی اس لئے مراد نہیں ہو سکتا کہ حوادثات دنیا اگرچہ فی حد ذا تہا متناہیہ ہیں کیونکہ یہ دار التکلیف یعنی عالم دنیا خود منقضی ہے لیکن چونکہ یہ حوادثات کثیر التعداد ہیں اور جب تک دنیا قائم ہے یہ منقطع نہیں ہو سکتے لہذا یہ کسی حصر حاصر اور ضبط مجتہد کے تحت نہیں آسکتے۔ هو المعنی بعد اے لا تكاد تتناهی یعنی مصنف کے قول لا تكاد تناہی کی مراد یہی ہے کہ جب تک دنیا قائم ہے یہ حوادثات ختم نہیں ہوں گے جو کسی حصر و ضبط کے تحت نہیں آ سکتے یہ مراد نہیں ہے کہ نفس الامر کے لحاظ سے یہ امور حقیقتاً غیر متناہی ہیں فلا یعلم
Then his objection: 'Ahkam is not intended in the definition' – objection. That is, the Ash'aris who defined fiqh as 'knowledge of rulings' – this is an objection to that. Because by 'ahkam' it may mean either the totality (al-kull), i.e., the whole as a whole; or each individual (kull wāḥid), which would necessitate that a faqih must know every single ruling; or some with a specific proportion (ba‘ḍ lahu nisba mu‘ayyana), meaning some rulings that have a specific relation to all rulings, like half or more, such as two-thirds; or some absolutely (al-ba‘ḍ muṭlaqan), meaning absolutely some, even if few. And all these mentioned possibilities are invalid. As for the first (totality): it cannot be intended because the events of the world, although finite in themselves since this world is finite, yet because these events are extremely numerous and will not cease as long as the world exists, they cannot be contained by any encompassing enumeration or the control of a mujtahid. That is the meaning of the author's statement 'they are almost endless' – his intent is that as long as the world exists, these events will not end and cannot be encompassed. It does not mean that they are actually infinite in themselves. So it is not known.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probing the meaning of 'ahkam' (rulings) in the Ash'ari definition of fiqh. It lists five possible interpretations: (1) the totality of all rulings, (2) each ruling individually, (3) some rulings with a specific proportion to all rulings, (4) some rulings absolutely (even few), and (5) perhaps a fifth not stated? The text says 'all these possibilities are invalid'. It explains why the first (totality) is impossible: events are too numerous to be fully known by any mujtahid. The author's expression 'almost endless' refers to practical impossibility of comprehensive knowledge, not theoretical infinitude. This analysis shows the definition must be understood as referring to the method of deriving rulings (usul) rather than the rulings themselves.

Key Terms
الکل (al-kull): The totality of all rulings as a whole.
کل واحد (kull wāḥid): Each individual ruling.
بعض له نسبة معینة (ba‘ḍ lahu nisba mu‘ayyana): Some rulings with a specific proportion to all rulings, e.g., half or two-thirds.
بعض مطلق (ba‘ḍ muṭlaq): Absolutely some rulings, even few.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the first interpretation of 'ahkam' (the totality of all rulings) considered invalid?
A) Because rulings are infinite in themselves.
B) Because the events from which rulings arise are so numerous that they cannot be fully known by any mujtahid.
C) Because fiqh only deals with practical rulings, not theoretical ones.
D) Because the totality of rulings is known only by Allah.

Key Takeaway
The definition of fiqh must refer to the method of deriving rulings, not the rulings themselves, because complete knowledge of all rulings is humanly impossible.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
8 min
Defending the Definition of Fiqh: Analysis of 'All Rulings'
Hanafi usul commentary refuting objections to the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four possible interpretations of 'all rulings' in the definition of fiqh, explain why three are rejected, and apply the Hanafi resolution that fiqh is knowledge of the collective whole of rulings that actually occur.

Core Idea
Fiqh is knowledge of the collective whole of eligible rulings, not each infinite event.

The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of all practical sharia rulings from detailed evidence' faced a serious objection: if rulings are infinite, how can anyone know all of them? In this lesson, we follow a Hanafi commentator as he systematically refutes this objection by analyzing four possible meanings of 'all rulings'. We will see why three interpretations are impossible or absurd, and how the fourth—interpreted correctly—makes fiqh attainable. The key insight is the distinction between 'all rulings' as a collective whole versus 'every single ruling' individually, which resolves the infinite regress problem.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four interpretations
The passage considers four possibilities: (1) knowledge of every individual event's ruling, (2) knowledge of every single issue, (3) knowledge of a specific proportion of the whole, and (4) knowledge of any part, even a small one.
These are the possible understandings of 'all rulings' in the definition.
2
Analyze the rejection of the first two
Interpretation 1 is impossible because no human can encompass infinite events. Interpretation 2 is false because even Imam Malik, a great faqih, did not know many rulings (36 out of 40 he said 'la adri').
The second interpretation fails by empirical evidence.
3
Understand the rejection of the third and fourth
Interpretation 3 fails because if the whole is unknown in quantity, any fraction (half, most) remains unknown. Interpretation 4 is too permissive: knowing one or two rulings would make anyone a faqih, which is false.
The fourth is rejected because it trivializes fiqh.
4
Examine the collective vs. individual distinction
The commentator notes that the author's contrast between 'all rulings' (kull majmu'i) and 'every single ruling' (kull ifradi) is invalid because they are identical in meaning for knowledge: knowing the collective is knowing the individuals. Examples include 'the people lift a stone' (collective only) and 'each person suffices with this food' (individual only), but knowledge of rulings is like 'I struck the people' (both apply).
This resolves the apparent conflict between infinite and finite.
5
Apply the resolution to the definition
The resolution: 'all rulings' meant as the sum of past and future rulings (infinite) is indeed unknowable, but the definition intends the holistic knowledge of the rulings that actually occur and come before a mujtahid. Thus fiqh remains possible.
The definition is defended by specifying the referent.

احكامها جوئیا یعنی ان تمام حوادثات کے احکام جزی جزی کا علم حاصل نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ یہ کسی بشر کے احاطہ علمی میں آہی نہیں سکتے ولا کلیا اور نہ ان کا علم کلی تفصیلی ہو سکتا ہے یعنی یہ تمام امور کسی ضابطہ" کلیہ کے تحت درج ہوں جس سے ان احکام کی تفصیل معلوم کی جاسکے ایسے بھی نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ ان حوادثات میں اتنا اختلاف ہے کہ کسی ایک ضابطہ کلیہ کے تحت درج نہیں ہو سکتے تو اس صورت میں پھر کوئی ایک بھی فقیہ نہیں ہو سکتا۔

وههنا بحث حاصل بحث یہ ہے کہ مصنف نے جو کل الاحکام کی شق کو بمقابل كل واحد من الاحکام پیش کیا ہے یہ تقابل صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ ان دونوں شقوق میں تغایر نہیں بلکہ آپس میں متحد ہیں اس کی تفصیل کرتے ہوئے شارح نے کہا من الاحكام ما يصح الم یعنی بعض احکام ایسے ہوتے ہیں کہ ان کا حمل کل مجموعی طور پر صحیح ہوتا ہے اور کل افرادی یعنی کل واحد پر ان کا حمل کرنا صحیح نہیں ہوتا کھولنا كل القوم يرفع هذا الحجر یعنی کل قوم بحیثیت مجموع من حیث المجموع اس پتھر کو اٹھا سکتی ہے لا کل واحد منھم یعنی ہر ایک ایک فرد انفرادی طور پر نہیں اٹھا سکتا ومنها ما هو بالعكس بعض احکام بالعکس ہیں یعنی ان کا حمل کل انفرادی پر سیح ہوتا ہے اور کل مجموعی پر صحیح نہیں ہوتا کھولنا كل واحد من الناس يكفيه هذا الطعام يعني يه طعام لوگوں میں سے ہر ایک ایک کو کافی ہو سکتا ہے یعنی ایک انسان منفردًا کھائے تو اسے کافی ہو گا لاكل الناس یعنی مجموعہ لوگوں کے لئے کافی نہیں ہے ومنها ما لا يختلت یعنی بعض احکام ایسے ہوتے ہیں کہ کل مجموعی اور کل افرادی کے لحاظ سے مختلف نہیں ہوتے بلکہ دونوں معنی کے لحاظ سے علی سبیل السو یہ صحیح قرار پاتے ہیں کھو لنا ضربت كل القوم اوكل واحد منهم یعنی بہت كل القوم میں خواه کل مجموعی یا کل افرادی دونوں معنی یکساں طور پر صحیح ہیں کیونکہ ایسے احکام میں ان کے اندر تغایر نہیں بلکہ استحاد ہوتا ہے مجموع من حیث المجموع افراد قوم کو مارنا یہ بعینہ ہر ایک ایک کو مارنا ہے و بالعکس تو یہاں ان دونوں میں کوئی تغایر نہیں ہے۔
ومعرفة الاحكام من هذا القبيل یعنی احکام کی معرفت اس قبیل ثانی سے ہے یعنی کل مجموعی اور کل افرادی کے لحاظ سے مختلف اور متغائر المعنی نہیں ہیں بلکہ ان کے اندر اتحاد ہے کیونکہ معرفت جمیع کی کل واحد کی معرفت کے ساتھ حاصل ہوتی ہے اور کل واحد کی معرفت جمیع کی معرفت ہے جب ان میں اتحاد ہوا تو ان کے مابین تقابل دنیا صحیح نہیں ہے وہ ان التزم اگر مصنف جوابا یہ التزام کرے کہ جمیع احکام کی معرفت سے مراد عام ہے خواہ کل واحد کی ہو یا فقط بعض کی جن کے ضمن میں مقصود حاصل ہو سکے تو فعدم تناهى الحوادث یعنی اس صورت میں جو عدم تنا ہی حوادث کو ان کی معرفت کے منافی قرار دیا ہے وہ صحیح نہیں کیونکہ عدم تنا ہی معرفتِ کل کے منافی ہے معرفت بعض کے منافی نہیں ہے والظاهر انه قصد یہ اس بحث مذکور کا جواب ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ کل مجموعی سے مراد مجموع احکام ماضیہ و آتیه یعنی گذشتہ اور آئندہ آنے والے سب مراد ہیں وبكل واحد یعنی کل واحد جو کہ کل افرادی ہے اس سے مراد ہر وہ حکم ہے جو وقوع پذیر ہو کر داخل فی الوجود ہو اور ذہن مجتہد کو بھی التفات کرنے کا موقع ملے تو اس وقت میں ان دونوں میں تغایر ہو گا اور تقابل ان کے مابین صحیح ہوگا ۔ حیث علل کیونکہ اس نے اول یعنی کل مجموعی کے مراد نہ ہونے کی تعلیل لاتنا ہی کے ساتھ پیش کی ہے تو لا تنا ہی جمیع امور ماضیہ وآتیہ کے مراد لینے میں میں ہو سکتی ہے اگر چہ صرف آتیہ مراد ہوتے تو یہ بھی غیر متناہیہ ہیں بمعنی لا تقف عند حد جو کسی ضبط ز حصر کے تحت نہیں آسکتے لیکن چونکہ یہاں لاتنا ہی حقیقی تو ہو نہیں سکتی کیونکہ یہ دار التکلیف جو محل حوادث یہ خود مقتضی اور فانی ہے لہذا مجموع احکام ماضیہ وہ آتیہ لزوم لاتنا ہی میں اظہر تھے اس لئے مجموعہ کا ذکر کر دیا۔ و الثاني يعنی کل واحد من انا حکام کے مراد نہ ہونے کی علتہ پیش کی ثبوت لا ادری کے ساتھ
Knowledge of the individual rulings of all events cannot be attained because it is beyond any human's capacity to encompass, nor can it be known collectively in detail such that all matters could be subsumed under a single universal principle from which the details of these rulings could be deduced, because the differences among these events are such that they cannot be subsumed under a single universal principle. In that case, no one could be a faqih. As for the second possibility, that he knows the ruling of every single issue, this is also incorrect because some people who are unanimously considered fuqaha did not know some rulings presented to them. For example, Imam Malik was asked about forty issues and replied 'I do not know' to thirty-six. As for the third possibility, a part with a specific relation to the whole, this also cannot be intended because when the whole is unknown in quantity and magnitude, any fractions attributed to that unknown whole will also be unknown. For example, half or third of the whole cannot be known unless the whole is known. Therefore, 'most rulings' also cannot be intended, because 'most' means more than half, and if half is unknown, how can 'most' be known? As for the fourth possibility, an absolute part, even if few, this also cannot be intended; otherwise, a person who knows one or two rulings from a proof would become a faqih, which is not the case. So all these divisions and possibilities are invalid, and therefore the mentioned definition of fiqh is subject to objection. The author did not explicitly mention this fourth possibility here, but it was mentioned earlier, and he only indicated it with the word 'thumma' (then), meaning after excluding the part even if small, nothing remains except the whole, etc. There is a discussion here: the author's presenting the 'all rulings' clause in opposition to 'every single ruling' is not correct because there is no distinction between the two; rather they are identical. The commentator elaborated: 'Among rulings, there are those to which the term 'all' applies collectively but not individually, e.g., 'all the people lift this stone' meaning the group collectively can lift it, not each individual. Some rulings are the opposite: the term 'all' applies individually but not collectively, e.g., 'each individual person is sufficed by this food' meaning each person individually, not the whole group. Some rulings are such that the term 'all' is not different whether applied collectively or individually; rather both meanings are equally valid, e.g., 'I struck all the people' or 'each one of them' because there is no distinction; striking the group as a whole is exactly striking each individual. Knowledge of rulings is of this latter type: the collective and individual meanings are not different but identical, because knowledge of all is attained through knowledge of each, and knowledge of each is knowledge of all. Since they are identical, opposing them is not correct. If the author insists in response that 'knowledge of all rulings' means general, whether it is knowledge of each or only of some by which the purpose is achieved, then the non-exhaustive nature of events is not contradictory because non-exhaustion negates knowledge of all, not knowledge of some. Apparently, this is the response to the above discussion. In summary, 'all collectively' means the sum of past and future rulings, and 'each individual' means every ruling that occurs and comes into existence and is attended to by the mujtahid's mind. At that point, there is a distinction between the two, and opposing them is valid. Because he (the author) justified the non-intention of the first (all collectively) by non-exhaustion, and non-exhaustion can only apply when intending all matters past and future, although if only future matters are intended, they are also non-exhaustive in that they cannot be confined to a limit. But here, true non-exhaustion is not possible because the abode of obligation is finite and perishable, so the sum of past and future rulings is more manifestly non-exhaustive; hence he mentioned the sum. And the second (each individual) he justified by the existence of 'I do not know' (instances of ignorance).
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This is a Hanafi usul commentary defending the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the practical sharia rulings derived from detailed evidence' against objections. The objector argues that 'knowledge of all rulings' is impossible because events are infinite. The commentator (Sharih) analyzes four possible interpretations of 'all rulings': (1) knowledge of every individual incident's ruling — impossible; (2) knowledge of every single issue — false because even Imam Malik did not know many; (3) a specific proportion of the whole — invalid because the whole is unknown; (4) any part, even small — would make a person with one or two rulings a faqih, which is false. Thus all four are rejected. Then a further discussion arises: the author of the matn (al-Tawdih) contrasted 'all rulings' (al-ahkam kullaha) with 'every single ruling' (kull wahid min al-ahkam), but the commentator notes these are not distinct; they are identical in meaning because knowledge of the collective is nothing but knowledge of the individuals. He gives examples: some things are collectively true but not individually (a stone lifted by a group), some individually true but not collectively (food sufficient for one), and some are both collectively and individually identical (striking a group). Knowledge of rulings belongs to the third type: knowing all rulings is the same as knowing each ruling. Therefore the contrast is invalid. The resolution is that by 'all rulings' the author meant the general body of sharia rulings (past, present, future), and by 'every single ruling' he meant those actual rulings that come before a mujtahid's mind. In that case, there is a distinction: the total set of all rulings is infinite and unknowable, while the individual rulings encountered are finite and knowable. This reconciles the definition.

Key Terms
کل مجموعی (kull majmu'i): The whole considered as a collective unit, e.g., 'all the people' as a group.
کل افرادی (kull ifradi): Each individual considered separately, e.g., 'every single person'.
فقیہ (faqih): One who knows the practical sharia rulings from detailed evidence, either potentially or actually.
لا ادری (la adri): Statement of ignorance; used as evidence that even great jurists do not know every ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the interpretation of 'all rulings' as 'every single ruling individually' (kull wahid) rejected?
A) Because it is impossible for any person to know every ruling since events are infinite.
B) Because Imam Malik said 'I do not know' to 36 out of 40 questions, proving even great jurists do not know all.
C) Because 'most rulings' are unknown.
D) Because the collective whole is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is knowledge of the practical sharia rulings as a collective whole, knowable through its parts, not as an infinite set of individual events.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Collective vs Individual Totality in Knowledge of Rulings
Explains how 'knowledge of rulings' relates to collective and individual totality and critiques Ibn al-Hajib's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between collective totality (kull mujamma‘) and individual totality (kull fardī) in the context of defining fiqh, and explain why the Hanafi school considers them identical when applied to knowledge of actual rulings.

Core Idea
Knowing all rulings collectively is identical to knowing each one individually — the distinction only matters for potential, not actual rulings.

This passage from a Hanafi sharh on Tawdih and Talwih addresses a critical point in the definition of fiqh: must a faqih know all rulings collectively or individually? The author clarifies that in actual knowledge, the two are the same, and the problem of infinite events only applies to potential rulings. This sets the stage for a debate with Ibn al-Hajib on whether fiqh can be defined as readiness rather than actual knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of totality
Distinguish between collective totality (kull mujamma‘) — all rulings as a whole — and individual totality (kull fardī) — each ruling separately. Note that the author says rulings differ as to whether these two are distinct or identical.
2
Apply to knowledge of rulings
According to the author, knowledge of rulings belongs to the category where collective and individual are identical. Explain why: knowing all rulings collectively requires knowing each one individually, and vice versa.
3
Analyze the non-exhaustibility objection
The objection that unlimited events makes knowledge of all rulings impossible applies only to collective totality of potential rulings, not to actual known rulings. The author argues that fiqh concerns actual occurrences.
4
Connect to the definition of fiqh
The Hanafi definition of fiqh as knowledge (ma‘rifa) of practical legal rulings from detailed evidence is therefore defensible; it does not require knowing infinite potential rulings.

میں سے ہر ایک ایک کو کافی ہو سکتا ہے یعنی ایک انسان منفردًا کھائے تو اسے کافی ہو گا لاکل الناس یعنی مجموعہ لوگوں کے لئے کافی نہیں ہے ومنها ما لا يختلت یعنی بعض احکام ایسے ہوتے ہیں کہ کل مجموعی اور کل کی معرفت کے ساتھ حاصل ہوتی ہے اور کل واحد کی معرفت جمیع کی معرفت ہے جب ان میں اتحاد ہوا تو ان کے مابین تقابل دنیا صحیح نہیں ہے وہ ان التزم اگر مصنف جوابا یہ التزام کرے کہ جمیع احکام کی معرفت سے مراد عام ہے خواہ کل واحد کی ہو یا فقط بعض کی جن کے ضمن میں مقصود حاصل ہو سکے تو فعدم تناهى الحوادث یعنی اس صورت میں جو عدم تنا ہی حوادث کو ان کی معرفت کے منافی قرار دیا ہے وہ صحیح نہیں کیونکہ عدم تنا ہی صحیح ہوگا ۔ حیث علل کیونکہ اس نے اول یعنی کل مجموعی کے مراد نہ ہونے کی تعلیل لاتنا ہی کے ساتھ پیش کی ہے تو لا تنا ہی جمیع امور ماضیہ وآتیہ کے مراد لینے میں میں ہو سکتی ہے اگر چہ صرف آتیہ مراد ہوتے تو یہ بھی غیر متناہیہ ہیں بمعنی لا تقف عند حد جو کسی ضبط ز حصر کے تحت نہیں آسکتے لیکن چونکہ یہاں لاتنا ہی حقیقی تو ہو نہیں سکتی کیونکہ یہ دار التکلیف جو محل حوادث یہ خود مقتضی اور فانی ہے لہذا مجموع احکام ماضیہ وہ آتیہ لزوم لاتنا ہی میں اظہر تھے اس لئے مجموعہ کا ذکر کر دیا۔ و الثاني يعنی کل واحد من انا حکام کے مراد نہ ہونے کی علتہ پیش کی ثبوت لا ادری کے ساتھ تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ اکل واحد سے وہ احکام مراد ہیں جو عالم وقوع میں واقع ہو کہ مجتہد کے سامنے پیش ہوئے اور انہوں نے لاادری کہا تو مراد اس سے واضح ہو گئی تو اس صورت میں کل مجموعی اور کل افرادی کے مابین تغایر ثابت ہوا لہذا ان کے مابین تقابل صحیح ہے ۔ حقیقت حال یہ ہے کہ مصنف کی مراد حقیقتاً ان دونوں شقوق سے یہی تھی جو شارح نے والظاہر کے ساتھ پیش کی ہے بلاوجہ شارح نے طوالت اختیار کی پھر آخر کار ادھر ہی رجوع کیا جو حقیقتاً مراد مصنف تھی تو یہ تطویل بلا فائدہ ہے البتہ اتنا فائدہ ضرور ہوا ہے کہ کل مجموعی اور کل افرادی کے کچھ احکام واضح ہو گئے ہیں ۔ و لما اجاب ابن الحاجب ان ابن حاجب نے اس اعتراض سے یہ جواب دیا کہ فقہ کی تعریف میں علم بمجموع الاحکام مراد ہے اور علم بمجموع الاحکام کا معنی ہے التهيؤ لذلك یعنی مجموع احکام کے معلوم کرنے کی صلاحیت اور استعداد موجود ہو رده المصنف مصنف نے اسے رد کر دیا کہ تہو۔ بعید تو غیر فقیہ کو بھی حاصل ہے اور تہیں قریب غیر مضبوط ہے یعنی اس کی کوئی خاص مقدار لینا اس کا کوئی ضابطہ نہیں ہے کہ استعداد و صلاحیت کی کتنا مقدار کو تہیوہ قریب کہا جاتا ہے لہذا یہ مجہول المقدار ہے ولما فسر التهيوء ابن حاجب نے اس تہیور قریب کی تفسیر کی يكون الشخص بحيث یعنی ایک شخص کا بائیں حیثیت و مرتبہ ہونا کہ ہر ایک ایک حادثہ کا حکم اجتہاد سے معلوم کر لے لاستجماعه المأخذ يعنی حکم معلوم کرنے کے لئے جتنا ماخذ اور اسباب و شرائط ہیں جن کے ساتھ تحصیل احکام پر تمکن و قدرت حاصل ہوتی ہے اور ان کی طرف رجوع کرنا معرفت احکام کے لئے کافی ہو ان تمام کا وہ شخص جامع ہو۔ ماخذ اور اسباب وغیرہ سے مراد یہ ہے ان يحصل لہ من مواد الاجتهاد واسبابها من العربية من القواعد الصرفية والنحوية وعلم القرآن والحديث واصولها مع تيقظ الخاطر و معرفة اسرار احكام الشريعة - رده المصنف مصنف نے اس تفسیر کو اربعہ وجوہ کے ساتھ رد کر دیا ہے يمكن الجواب یعنی جن اربعہ وجوہ کے ساتھ رد کیا ہے ان سے جواب دینا ممکن ہے تو شارح نے ان جوابات کی تفصیل کرتے ہوئے کہا بانا لا نسلم یہ مصنف کے پہلے دو وجوہ ایک یہ کہ علماء مجتہدین کو ساری زندگی بعض احکام کا علم نہ ہو سکا۔ دوسرا یہ کہ اجتہاد میں خطا بھی ہو جاتی ہے تو نہیں۔ قریب نے معرفہ احکام کا فائدہ نہ دیا تو شارح نے ان کو رد کیا کہ ہم تسلیم ہی نہیں کرتے کہ ان عدم تيسر معرفة یعنی بعض احکام کی معرفہ کا تیسر نہ ہونا اور خطار فی الاجتہاد تہیور بالمعنی المذکور کے منافی ہے اسے ہم تسلیم ہی نہیں کرتے لجوانان یکون کیونکہ ہو سکتا ہے کہ یہ عدم تیسر اور خطار با وجوہ تینوں قریب
From each one can be sufficient — i.e., if a single person eats alone, it suffices him, but it is not sufficient for the totality of people. And among them are those that do not differ — i.e., some rulings do not change with respect to collective totality and individual totality; rather, they are valid in both senses equally. For example, 'I struck the entire tribe' or 'I struck each one of them' — in 'I struck the entire tribe', whether collective or individual, both meanings are equally valid because in such rulings there is no difference but rather identity. Striking the tribe collectively is precisely striking each individual, and vice versa, so there is no difference here. And knowledge of rulings is of this second type — i.e., knowledge of rulings does not differ in meaning as collective or individual; rather, there is identity in them because knowledge of the collective is attained through knowledge of each individual, and knowledge of each individual is knowledge of the collective. When there is identity, comparison between them is not valid. If the author (Mussanif) responds by stipulating that 'knowledge of all rulings' refers to general knowledge whether of each individual or only some by which the purpose is achieved, then the non-exhaustibility of events does not contradict such knowledge because non-exhaustibility contradicts knowledge of the collective, not knowledge of some. And apparently, that is what is intended as the answer to this discussion. In summary: 'collective totality' means the totality of past and future rulings, and 'each individual' — individual totality — means every ruling that has actually occurred and entered existence and that the mujtahid's mind has attended to. Then there will be a difference between them, and comparison between them is valid. Because the author (Mussanif) gave as the reason for not intending the first (collective totality) the non-exhaustibility, but non-exhaustibility can occur when intending all past and future matters. Even if only future matters were intended, they are also non-exhaustible in the sense that they have no limit and cannot be confined, but here true non-exhaustibility cannot apply because the abode of obligation (dar al-taklif) is itself finite and perishable. Therefore, the totality of past and future rulings was more evidently subject to non-exhaustibility, so he mentioned the totality. The second — i.e., each individual — he explained the non-intention by the occurrence of 'I don't know'. So it becomes clear that by 'each individual' he means rulings that have actually occurred and been presented to the mujtahid, and he said 'I don't know' — then the meaning is clarified. So in this case, the difference between collective and individual totality is established, thus comparison between them is valid. The reality is that the author's intention from these two divisions was what the commentator presented with 'apparently', but the commentator unnecessarily prolonged the discussion and then returned to it, which is useless lengthening. However, this much benefit was gained: some rulings about collective and individual totality became clear. And when Ibn al-Hajib responded to this objection by saying that in the definition of fiqh, 'knowledge of all rulings' is intended, and 'knowledge of all rulings' means 'readiness (al-ta'ahhi') for that' — i.e., the ability and preparedness to know all rulings — the author (Mussanif) rejected it, saying that remote readiness is also possessed by a non-jurist, and close readiness is not well-defined — i.e., there is no specific measure for it; how much ability and preparedness is called close readiness? So it is of unknown measure. When Ibn al-Hajib interpreted 'close readiness' as 'a person being in a state such that he can derive knowledge of every incident through ijtihad, because he has gathered the sources' — i.e., all the sources, causes, and conditions through which capability and power to obtain rulings are achieved, and referring to them is sufficient for knowledge of rulings — the author rejected this interpretation with four points that can be answered. The commentator, detailing these answers, said: 'We do not accept' — this is the author's first two points: one being that scholars/mujtahids during their entire lives could not know some rulings; the second being that ijtihad sometimes involves error. Thus, close readiness did not yield knowledge of rulings. The commentator refuted these: 'We do not accept that the non-availability of knowledge of some rulings and error in ijtihad contradicts close readiness in the mentioned sense, because it is possible that these non-availability and error are not contrary to it.'
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) clarifying a subtle point in defining fiqh as 'knowledge of rulings' (ma'rifat al-ahkam). The discussion revolves around two types of totality: collective totality (kull al-mujamma') meaning all rulings collectively, and individual totality (kull al-fardi) meaning each ruling individually. The author argues that in the case of knowledge of rulings, these two are actually identical — knowing all rulings collectively is nothing but knowing each one individually, and vice versa. Therefore, the usual opposition between them does not apply. However, when it comes to the impossibility of knowing all rulings due to their infinite number (non-exhaustibility), the author clarifies that this impossibility pertains only to the collective totality, not to individual rulings that actually occur before the mujtahid. Ibn al-Hajib attempted to define fiqh as 'readiness' (al-ta'ahhi') to know rulings, but the Hanafi author (Mussanif) rejects this because 'readiness' is either too vague (remote) or insufficiently defined. Ibn al-Hajib further specifies 'close readiness' as possessing the tools for ijtihad, but the author counters with four objections, including that even great scholars may not know some rulings and that ijtihad may err. The commentator (Sharih) tries to defend Ibn al-Hajib by arguing that these do not necessarily negate readiness. The passage follows the typical Hanafi usul approach of rigorous semantic and logical analysis before settling on a definition. Note that this is a commentary on the Tawdih and Talwih; the 'Mussanif' likely refers to Sadr al-Shari'ah and the 'Sharih' to Taftazani, though the precise identity is debated. The Hanafi position is that fiqh is knowledge of practical legal rulings derived from detailed evidence, and that this knowledge is attainable despite the theoretical infinity of events.

Key Terms
کل مجموعی (kull al-mujamma‘): The collective totality of all rulings (past and future) taken as a whole
کل افرادی (kull al-fardī): Each individual ruling considered separately, especially those that occur and become known
التهيؤ القريب (al-ta’ahhu’ al-qarīb): Close readiness — a state where one has acquired the tools of ijtihad such that one can derive rulings as events arise

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the passage conclude about the relationship between knowing all rulings collectively and knowing each ruling individually for the purpose of defining fiqh?
A) They are distinct and opposing.
B) They are identical when considering actual rulings.
C) They are identical only for potential rulings.
D) The collective is broader than the individual.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between actual and potential rulings, establishing that fiqh as actual knowledge of rulings is possible because collective and individual totality of actual rulings are identical.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Does 'Ilm in Fiqh Definition Mean Faculty or Perception?
Resolving whether 'ilm means faculty or perception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between malaka and idrak in the Hanafi definition of fiqh and apply the rule that when 'ilm has an object it denotes perception of that object.

Core Idea
In fiqh definition, 'ilm means perception (idrak) of rulings, not a faculty (malaka), because it is qualified by an object.

This passage presents a critical refinement: the author defends that 'ilm in the definition of fiqh ('knowledge of the practical Sharia rulings from their detailed evidences') refers to the act of perceiving specific rulings, not a general faculty. The commentator had argued that 'ilm could mean malaka, citing common usage and an analogy with life. By analyzing the qualification by the object 'rulings' and the phrase 'from its detailed evidences', the author establishes that the definition requires actual perception of each ruling, not merely the capacity to derive them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing interpretations
Recognize that the dispute is over whether 'ilm in the definition of fiqh means malaka (a firm faculty enabling easy derivation) or idrak (the act of perceiving specific rulings). The commentator leans toward malaka; the author argues for idrak.
This distinction affects whether fiqh is seen as a disposition or as actual knowledge.
2
Understand the commentator's evidence
The commentator uses two arguments: (1) 'ilm is commonly applied to malaka, as when one says 'knowledge of such and such' meaning expertise; (2) the saying 'al-ilm ka al-hayat' (knowledge is like life) likens 'ilm to life as a means of perception, implying malaka.
The analogy compares 'ilm to life being a cause of perception, which would be malaka.
3
Analyze the author's response: qualification by an object
The author argues that when 'ilm has a specified object (here 'rulings'), it can only denote the act of perceiving that object, not a general faculty. Absolute 'ilm may allow malaka, but qualified 'ilm forces idrak.
This is a key usul principle: a word's meaning can shift based on context and qualification.
4
Consider the second evidence: 'from its detailed evidences'
The phrase 'from its detailed evidences' indicates that the knowledge is acquired through evidence for each ruling. Such acquired knowledge is necessarily perception (idrak) of individual rulings, not a pre-existing faculty.
This further supports that 'ilm here means the act of knowing each ruling via its proof.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author prefers idrak because the definition explicitly includes the object 'ahkam' and the phrase 'min adillatiha al-tafṣiliyya'. The commentator's analogy is rejected as applying only to absolute 'ilm, not to the qualified form here.
The tarjih is based on textual qualification and the nature of evidence-derived knowledge.

And we do not accept that the word 'ilm' has no indication—the author responds to the objection that the word 'ilm' does not indicate specific tahawwur. The objection was that the word 'ilm' does not indicate specific tahawwur at all. The author refutes: We do not accept that there is no indication. The summary is: We do not accept that the word 'ilm' does not indicate specific tahawwur, because by tahawwur is meant the malaka (faculty) with which one gains the ability to perceive particular rulings. And applying 'ilm' to this malaka is widespread in custom. For example, in defining and knowing something, one says 'knowledge of such and such', so according to the verifiers, it is this malaka that is meant. Similarly, one says 'industry'. And 'ilm' does not mean the very act of perception. Also, the saying 'knowledge is like life'—here the aspect of comparison is being a means and cause. That is, just as life is a means and cause of perception, so knowledge is a means and cause. So in this analogy, 'ilm' means the malaka, not the act of perception, because that which is a means and cause can only be a malaka, not the act of perception. The response to this refutation of the commentator is that the application of 'ilm' to the malaka is widespread when the word 'ilm' is used absolutely and its object (the known) is neither mentioned nor implied. When a specific object is mentioned or implied with 'ilm', then only the meaning of perception of that specific thing is intended, not the malaka. And here in the definition of fiqh, it is said 'knowledge of the rulings', and the object 'rulings' is mentioned. Therefore, here only perception can be intended, not malaka. In the analogy of 'knowledge is like life', since 'ilm' is absolute there, malaka can be intended. In the definition of fiqh, the word 'ilm' is not absolute but qualified by an object, so taking it as malaka is incorrect. Some have also said that in 'knowledge is like life', 'ilm' does not mean malaka but rather the principles and rules, because they too are a means and cause, just as life is a means of perception. Hence, the commentator's using this analogy is incorrect. Moreover, the qualification in the definition of fiqh 'from its detailed evidences' also determines that here 'ilm' means perception, not malaka. Because it is stated that the phrase 'from its evidences' is related to 'ilm', so the knowledge that is acquired from evidence is perception of the thing, not a malaka of the thing. Therefore, the author's...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage examines whether the word 'ilm' in the definition of fiqh ('knowledge of the practical Sharia rulings from their detailed evidences') denotes a malaka (faculty) or an act of perception. The author defends the view that it means perception, not malaka, against the commentator's objection. The commentator argued that 'ilm' can mean malaka, citing common usage and the analogy 'knowledge is like life'. The author responds: When 'ilm' has a specified object (like 'rulings'), it refers to the act of perceiving that object, not a general faculty. In the analogy, 'ilm' is absolute, so it may indicate malaka, but in the definition it is qualified. Therefore, the definition's 'ilm' must mean perception. This is further supported by the phrase 'from its detailed evidences': the knowledge derived from evidence is the perception of each ruling, not a lasting faculty. Hence, the definition accurately describes the act of knowing rulings via evidence, not the capacity to do so.

Key Terms
الملكة (al-malaka): A firm faculty or disposition enabling the easy derivation of rulings.
الإدراك (al-idrak): The act of perception or cognition of a specific object.
التهور (كما يظهر) (al-tahawwur): The specific determination of knowledge; here it refers to the object of knowledge, and by tahawwur is meant malaka.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that 'ilm in the definition of fiqh means perception (idrak) rather than faculty (malaka)?
A) Because malaka is not a valid concept in usul
B) Because the word 'ilm is qualified by the object 'rulings', so it denotes the act of knowing them
C) Because all definitions of 'ilm must refer to perception
D) Because the analogy of life requires malaka

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi definition of fiqh, 'ilm is understood as the perception (idrak) of specific rulings from their detailed evidences, not as a general faculty (malaka).

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
10 min
Definition of Fiqh: Knowledge and Faculty of Istinbat
Author's definition of fiqh as knowledge with istinbat faculty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fiqh and explain why both knowledge of revealed rulings and the faculty of istinbat are necessary.

Core Idea
Fiqh is not mere knowledge—it is the ability to derive rulings from evidences (malakat al-istinbat).

This passage from al-Tawdih presents a precise definition of fiqh. It distinguishes between simply knowing rulings and being a true faqih who can derive new rulings from the sources. The author emphasizes that both knowledge of the revealed rulings and the faculty (malakah) of correct istinbat are essential. Let's unpack the conditions and see how this definition applies to the Companions and to later jurists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of fiqh
Locate where the author states that fiqh is knowledge of rulings plus the faculty of istinbat. Note the phrase 'with the faculty of correctly deriving rulings from evidences.'
2
Understand why knowledge alone is insufficient
The author uses the example of the Companions: they knew Arabic and understood the revealed rulings, but not all were called fuqaha. Only those who could derive rulings (istinbat) qualified. This shows that knowledge without istinbat faculty does not make one a faqih.
Istinbat is key to being a mujtahid.
3
Examine the exception for analogical rulings (qiyas)
The text states that knowing analogical rulings (al-masa'il al-qiyasiyya) is not a condition for being a faqih; otherwise, circularity would occur. Instead, the faculty of istinbat is the condition, and the actual analogical rulings are its product.
This prevents a logical loop: fiqh cannot depend on its own results.
4
Apply the definition to the Companions
The Companions at the time of revelation had knowledge of the rulings revealed up to their time. Those who possessed the faculty of istinbat were the fuqaha among them. Later rulings and consensus issues are additional knowledge, not conditions for their fiqh.
Consensus (ijma) did not exist during the Prophet's lifetime, so it cannot be a condition for the Companions' fiqh.
5
Synthesize the complete definition
Combine all elements: fiqh is (1) knowledge of all practical shar'i rulings that have been revealed, (2) acquired from detailed evidences, and (3) accompanied by the faculty (malakah) of correctly deriving rulings (istinbat) from those evidences.
This definition is specifically Hanafi; other schools may vary.

قال المصنف فى التوضيح بل هو العلم بكل الاحكام الشرعية العملية التي قد ظهر نزول الوحی بھا الا مصنف نے اپنی طرف سے یہ تعریف فقہ کی پیش کی ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ فقہ ان تمام احکام شرعیہ پر اجماع علم کو کہا جاتا ہے جس کے ساتھ نزول وحی ظاہر ہو چکا ہو اور جن احکام شرعیہ پر اجوااع متعقد ہو چکا ہو ان تمام کا علم جو حاصل ادلہ تفصیلیہ سے ہو ایسے علم کو فقہ کہا جاتا ہے بشرطیکہ احکام کا ادلہ سے صحیح استنباط کرنے کا ملکہ بھی ہو یا فروع قیاسیہ کے استنباط من الاحكام الم المنصوصہ کا ملکہ ہو ۔ فالمعتبر ان يعلم یعنی ان جمیع احکام سے مراد وہ ہیں جو بوقت ظہور وحی کے جمع ہوں یعنی چونکہ وحی تو تدریجاً وقتا فوقتا نازل ہوتی رہی ہے تو جس وقت ان اوقات مختلفہ میں جتنا جمیع ہوتے گئے ان کا علم حاصل ہو۔ فالصحابة رضى الله عنهم الم عني صحابه بوقت نزول وحی کے فقہاہر تھے اگر چہ بعض احکام بعد میں نازل ہوئے تو اس نزول بعد یہ سے پہلے جتنا احکام نازل ہو چکے تھے ان کا جاننا ضروری ہے اور بعد میں جو نازل ہوتے رہے ان کے ظہور کے بعد ان کا جاننا ضروری ہوگا۔ تو مایو یظھر یہ ظہر نزول الوحی بہا کی قید کا فائدہ پیش کر رہا ہے یعنی جب تک نزول وحی کا ظہور نہ ہو تو اس کا جاننا فقیہ کے لئے ضروری نہیں ہے۔ اور ظہور وچی سے قبل فقیہ کو علم بالاحکام النازلہ ہونا یہ ان کی فقاہتہ پر اثر انداز نہیں ہوگا ۔ و الصحابة لعربيتهم یہ ملکہ استنباط کی قید کا فائدہ ہے یعنی صحابہ چونکہ عربی اللسان تھے اس وجہ سے احکام نازلہ کا انہیں بوجہ غربی دان ہونے کے علم ہو جاتا تھا لیکن پھر بھی ہر صحابی کو فقیہ نہیں کہا گیا بلکہ صرف مستنبطین کو کہا گیا ہے تو
